Bengali</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Bengali</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90/: &#39;au&#39; in &#39;fraud&#39;">\xc9\x94\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> also known by its <a href="/wiki/Endonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Endonym">endonym</a> <b>Bangla</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/\xca\x8c/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;cut&#39;">\xca\x8c</span><span title="/\xc5\x8b/: &#39;ng&#39; in &#39;sing&#39;">\xc5\x8b</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\xac\xe0\xa6\xbe\xe0\xa6\x82\xe0\xa6\xb2\xe0\xa6\xbe</span>), is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Aryan language">Indo-Aryan language</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>. It is the official and most widely spoken language of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and second most widely spoken of the 22 <a href="/wiki/Scheduled_languages_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Scheduled languages of India">scheduled languages of India</a>, behind <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> is Modern Standard Bengali (Literary Bengali).<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It serves as the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the nation, with 98% of Bangladeshis being fluent in Bengali (including dialects) as their <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Within India, Bengali is the official language of the states of <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tripura" title="Tripura">Tripura</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Barak_Valley" title="Barak Valley">Barak Valley</a> in the state of <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>. It is also spoken in different parts of the Brahmaputra valley of Assam. It is also the most widely spoken language in the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_and_Nicobar_Islands" title="Andaman and Nicobar Islands">Andaman and Nicobar Islands</a> in the Bay of Bengal,<sup id="cite_ref-:1_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and is spoken by significant minorities in other states including <a href="/wiki/Jharkhand" title="Jharkhand">Jharkhand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mizoram" title="Mizoram">Mizoram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meghalaya" title="Meghalaya">Meghalaya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>. With approximately 250\xe2\x80\x93300<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million total speakers worldwide,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Bengali is usually counted as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">seventh most spoken native language in the world</a> by population.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EthnologueSp_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EthnologueSp-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dictionaries from the early 20th century attributed slightly more than half of the Bengali vocabulary to native words (i.e., naturally modified <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> words, corrupted forms of <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> words, and loanwords from non-Indo-European languages), about 30 percent to unmodified Sanskrit words, and the remainder to foreign words.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Dominant in the last group was <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, which was also the source of some grammatical forms. More recent studies suggest that the use of native and foreign words has been increasing, mainly because of the preference of Bengali speakers for the colloquial style.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bengali_literature" title="Bengali literature">Bengali literature</a>, with its <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bengali_literature" title="History of Bengali literature">millennium-old history</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladeshi_folk_literature" title="Bangladeshi folk literature">folk heritage</a>, has extensively developed since the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_renaissance" title="Bengali renaissance">Bengali renaissance</a> and is one of the most prominent and diverse literary traditions in Asia. Both the national anthems of Bangladesh (<i><a href="/wiki/Amar_Sonar_Bangla" class="mw-redirect" title="Amar Sonar Bangla">Amar Sonar Bangla</a></i>) and India (<i><a href="/wiki/Jana_Gana_Mana" title="Jana Gana Mana">Jana Gana Mana</a></i>) were composed in Bengali by <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Rabindranath Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a>. The first two verses of a patriotic song written in Bengali by <a href="/wiki/Bankim_Chandra_Chatterjee" title="Bankim Chandra Chatterjee">Bankim Chandra Chatterjee</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Vande_Mataram" title="Vande Mataram">Vande Mataram</a></i>, was adopted as the "national song" of India in both the colonial period and later in 1950 in independent India. Furthermore, it is believed by many that the national anthem of Sri Lanka (<a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka_Matha" title="Sri Lanka Matha">Sri Lanka Matha</a>) was inspired by a Bengali poem written by <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Rabindranath Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TOI030411_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TOI030411-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TH040216_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TH040216-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HT120510_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HT120510-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>  while some even believe the anthem was originally written in Bengali and then translated into <a href="/wiki/Sinhalese_language" title="Sinhalese language">Sinhalese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickramasinghe_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickramasinghe-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DM300315_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DM300315-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DS070511_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DS070511-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TH170511_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TH170511-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In 1952, the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_Language_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali Language Movement">Bengali Language Movement</a> successfully pushed for the language\'s official status in the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Pakistan" title="Dominion of Pakistan">Dominion of Pakistan</a>, allowing for education in and official use of the language. In 1999, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> recognized <a href="/wiki/Language_Movement_Day" title="Language Movement Day">21 February</a> as <a href="/wiki/International_Mother_Language_Day" title="International Mother Language Day">International Mother Language Day</a> in recognition of the language movement in <a href="/wiki/East_Bengal" title="East Bengal">East Bengal</a> (now Bangladesh). Language is an important element of <a href="/wiki/Bengali_nationalism" title="Bengali nationalism">Bengali identity</a> and binds together a <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Bengal" title="Culture of Bengal">culturally diverse</a> region.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coin_-_Silver_-_Circa_9-10th_Century_13th_Century_CE_-_Harikela_Kingdom_-_ACCN_90-C2752_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2014-04-04_4303.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Coin_-_Silver_-_Circa_9-10th_Century_13th_Century_CE_-_Harikela_Kingdom_-_ACCN_90-C2752_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2014-04-04_4303.JPG/220px-Coin_-_Silver_-_Circa_9-10th_Century_13th_Century_CE_-_Harikela_Kingdom_-_ACCN_90-C2752_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2014-04-04_4303.JPG" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Coin_-_Silver_-_Circa_9-10th_Century_13th_Century_CE_-_Harikela_Kingdom_-_ACCN_90-C2752_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2014-04-04_4303.JPG/330px-Coin_-_Silver_-_Circa_9-10th_Century_13th_Century_CE_-_Harikela_Kingdom_-_ACCN_90-C2752_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2014-04-04_4303.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1241" data-file-height="1241" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coin_-_Silver_-_Circa_9-10th_Century_13th_Century_CE_-_Harikela_Kingdom_-_ACCN_90-C2752_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2014-04-04_4303.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver coin with proto-Bengali script, <a href="/wiki/Harikela" title="Harikela">Harikela</a> Kingdom, circa 9th-13th century</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_language_of_Bengal">Ancient language of Bengal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ancient language of Bengal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> was spoken in Bengal since the <a href="/wiki/First_millennium_BCE" class="mw-redirect" title="First millennium BCE">first millennium BCE</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">Gupta Empire</a>, Bengal was a hub of <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_literature" title="Sanskrit literature">Sanskrit literature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Middle Indo-Aryan languages">Middle Indo-Aryan dialects</a> were spoken in Bengal in the <a href="/wiki/First_millennium" class="mw-redirect" title="First millennium">first millennium</a> when the region was a part of the <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> Realm. These dialects were called <a href="/wiki/Magadhi_Prakrit" title="Magadhi Prakrit">Magadhi Prakrit</a>. They eventually evolved into <a href="/wiki/Ardha_Magadhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ardha Magadhi">Ardha Magadhi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jain_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jain-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-keith_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keith-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Ardha Magadhi began to give way to what are called <a href="/wiki/Apabhra%E1%B9%83%C5%9Ba" title="Apabhra\xe1\xb9\x83\xc5\x9ba">Apabhra\xe1\xb9\x83\xc5\x9ba</a> languages at the end of the first millennium.<sup id="cite_ref-tb_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tb-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_of_Bengali">Emergence of Bengali</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Emergence of Bengali">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Along with other <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Indo-Aryan_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Indo-Aryan languages">Eastern Indo-Aryan languages</a>, Bengali evolved circa 1000\xe2\x80\x931200 AD from Sanskrit and Magadhi Prakrit.<sup id="cite_ref-Oberlies2007_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberlies2007-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The local Apabhra\xe1\xb9\x83\xc5\x9ba of the eastern subcontinent, Purbi Apabhra\xe1\xb9\x83\xc5\x9ba or <a href="/wiki/Abahatta" class="mw-redirect" title="Abahatta">Abahatta</a> ("Meaningless Sounds"), eventually evolved into regional dialects, which in turn formed three groups of the <a href="/wiki/Bengali%E2%80%93Assamese_languages" title="Bengali\xe2\x80\x93Assamese languages">Bengali\xe2\x80\x93Assamese languages</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bihari_languages" title="Bihari languages">Bihari languages</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Odia_language" title="Odia language">Odia language</a>. Some argue that the points of divergence occurred much earlier \xe2\x80\x94 going back to even 500,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> but the language was not static: different varieties coexisted and authors often wrote in multiple dialects in this period. For example, Ardhamagadhi is believed to have evolved into Abahatta around the 6th century, which competed with the ancestor of Bengali for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-abahatta_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abahatta-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Proto-Bengali was the language of the <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">Pala Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sena_dynasty" title="Sena dynasty">Sena dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Bengali">Middle Bengali</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle Bengali">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_coin_of_Danujamarddana.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Silver_coin_of_Danujamarddana.jpg/220px-Silver_coin_of_Danujamarddana.jpg" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Silver_coin_of_Danujamarddana.jpg/330px-Silver_coin_of_Danujamarddana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Silver_coin_of_Danujamarddana.jpg/440px-Silver_coin_of_Danujamarddana.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1614" data-file-height="814" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_coin_of_Danujamarddana.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver Taka from the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Bengal">Sultanate of Bengal</a>, circa 1417</div></div></div>\n<p>During the medieval period, Middle Bengali was characterized by the <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">elision</a> of word-final <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x85</span> <i>\xc3\xb4</i>, the spread of compound verbs and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> influences. Bengali was an official court language of the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Bengal">Sultanate of Bengal</a>. Muslim rulers promoted the literary development of Bengali.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Bengali became the most spoken <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> language in the Sultanate.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> This period saw borrowing of Perso-Arabic terms into Bengali vocabulary. Major texts of Middle Bengali (1400\xe2\x80\x931800) include <a href="/wiki/Chandidas" title="Chandidas">Chandidas</a>\' <i><a href="/wiki/Shreekrishna_Kirtana" title="Shreekrishna Kirtana">Shreekrishna Kirtana</a></i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Bengali">Modern Bengali</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Modern Bengali">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Central_Shaheed_Minar_-_Dhaka_Medical_College_Campus_-_Dhaka_2015-05-31_2584.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Central_Shaheed_Minar_-_Dhaka_Medical_College_Campus_-_Dhaka_2015-05-31_2584.JPG/220px-Central_Shaheed_Minar_-_Dhaka_Medical_College_Campus_-_Dhaka_2015-05-31_2584.JPG" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Central_Shaheed_Minar_-_Dhaka_Medical_College_Campus_-_Dhaka_2015-05-31_2584.JPG/330px-Central_Shaheed_Minar_-_Dhaka_Medical_College_Campus_-_Dhaka_2015-05-31_2584.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Central_Shaheed_Minar_-_Dhaka_Medical_College_Campus_-_Dhaka_2015-05-31_2584.JPG/440px-Central_Shaheed_Minar_-_Dhaka_Medical_College_Campus_-_Dhaka_2015-05-31_2584.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="6016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Central_Shaheed_Minar_-_Dhaka_Medical_College_Campus_-_Dhaka_2015-05-31_2584.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Shaheed_Minar,_Dhaka" title="Shaheed Minar, Dhaka">Central Shaheed Minar</a> in Dhaka commemorates the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_Language_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali Language Movement">Bengali Language Movement</a>. <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> commemorates the movement as <a href="/wiki/International_Mother_Language_Day" title="International Mother Language Day">International Mother Language Day</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The modern literary form of Bengali was developed during the 19th and early 20th centuries based on the dialect spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Nadia_district" title="Nadia district">Nadia region</a>, a west-central Bengali dialect. Bengali presents a strong case of <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a>, with the literary and standard form differing greatly from the colloquial speech of the regions that identify with the language.<sup id="cite_ref-cornell_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornell-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>  The modern <a href="/wiki/Bengali_vocabulary" title="Bengali vocabulary">Bengali vocabulary</a> contains the vocabulary base from Magadhi Prakrit and Pali, also <a href="/wiki/Tatsama" title="Tatsama">tatsamas</a> and reborrowings from Sanskrit and other major borrowings from Persian, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic_languages" title="Austroasiatic languages">Austroasiatic languages</a> and other languages in contact with.\n</p><p>During this period, \nthe\n</p>\n<ul><li><span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x9a\xe0\xa6\xb2\xe0\xa6\xbf\xe0\xa6\xa4\xe0\xa6\xad\xe0\xa6\xbe\xe0\xa6\xb7\xe0\xa6\xbe</span> <i>Ch\xc3\xb4lit\xc3\xb4bhasha</i> form of Bengali using simplified inflections and other changes, was emerging from</li>\n<li><span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\xb8\xe0\xa6\xbe\xe0\xa6\xa7\xe0\xa7\x81\xe0\xa6\xad\xe0\xa6\xbe\xe0\xa6\xb7\xe0\xa6\xbe</span> <i>Sadhubhasha</i> (Proper form or original form of Bengali) as the form of choice for written Bengali.<sup id="cite_ref-SKumar_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SKumar-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>In 1948 the Government of Pakistan tried to impose Urdu as the sole state language in Pakistan, starting the Bengali language movement.<sup id="cite_ref-sas_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sas-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bengali_Language_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali Language Movement">Bengali Language Movement</a> was a popular ethno-linguistic movement in the former <a href="/wiki/East_Bengal" title="East Bengal">East Bengal</a> (today <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>), which was a result of the strong linguistic consciousness of the <a href="/wiki/Bengalis" title="Bengalis">Bengalis</a> to gain and protect spoken and written Bengali\'s recognition as a state language of the then <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Pakistan" title="Dominion of Pakistan">Dominion of Pakistan</a>. On the day of 21 February 1952 five students and political activists were killed during protests near the campus of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Dhaka" title="University of Dhaka">University of Dhaka</a>. In 1956 Bengali was made a state language of Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-sas_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sas-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The day has since been observed as <a href="/wiki/Language_Movement_Day" title="Language Movement Day">Language Movement Day</a> in Bangladesh and was proclaimed <a href="/wiki/International_Mother_Language_Day" title="International Mother Language Day">International Mother Language Day</a> by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> on 17 November 1999. This gives Bengali the distinction of being the only language in the world that is known for its language movements and people sacrificing their lives for its preservation.\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Bengali_Language_Movement_(Barak_Valley)" title="Bengali Language Movement (Barak Valley)">Bengali language movement in the Indian state of Assam</a> took place in 1961, a protest against the decision of the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Assam" title="Government of Assam">Government of Assam</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Assamese_language" title="Assamese language">Assamese</a> the only official language of the state even though a significant proportion of the population were Bengali-speaking, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Barak_Valley" title="Barak Valley">Barak Valley</a>.\n</p><p>In 2010, the parliament of Bangladesh and the legislative assembly of West Bengal proposed that Bengali be made an official <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> language.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Their motions came after Bangladeshi Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Hasina" title="Sheikh Hasina">Sheikh Hasina</a> suggested the idea while addressing the <a href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="UN General Assembly">UN General Assembly</a> that year.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_distribution">Geographical distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geographical distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Bengali language is native to the region of <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>, which comprises Indian states of <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a> and the present-day nation of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brick_Lane_-_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Brick_Lane_-_London.jpg/220px-Brick_Lane_-_London.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Brick_Lane_-_London.jpg/330px-Brick_Lane_-_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Brick_Lane_-_London.jpg/440px-Brick_Lane_-_London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brick_Lane_-_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Bengali sign in <a href="/wiki/Brick_Lane" title="Brick Lane">Brick Lane</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, which is home to a large Bengali diaspora</div></div></div>\n<p>Besides the native region it is also spoken by the Bengalis living in <a href="/wiki/Tripura" title="Tripura">Tripura</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a> and the Bengali population in the Indian union territory of <a href="/wiki/Andaman_and_Nicobar_Islands" title="Andaman and Nicobar Islands">Andaman and Nicobar Islands</a>. Bengali is also spoken in the neighboring states of <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jharkhand" title="Jharkhand">Jharkhand</a>, and sizable minorities of Bengali speakers reside in Indian cities outside Bengal, including <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Varanasi" title="Varanasi">Varanasi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vrindavan" title="Vrindavan">Vrindavan</a>. There are also significant Bengali-speaking communities in the <a href="/wiki/Bangladeshis_in_the_Middle_East" title="Bangladeshis in the Middle East">Middle East</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali American">United States</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bangladeshis_in_Malaysia" title="Bangladeshis in Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladeshis_in_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bangladeshis in Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladeshi_Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Bangladeshi Canadian">Canada</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Bangladeshi" title="British Bangladeshi">United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladeshis_in_Italy" title="Bangladeshis in Italy">Italy</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Official status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/States_of_India_by_Bengali_speakers" title="States of India by Bengali speakers">States of India by Bengali speakers</a></div>\n<p>Bengali is <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national</a> and <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official</a> language of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, and one of the 23 <a href="/wiki/Languages_with_official_status_in_India" title="Languages with official status in India">official languages</a> in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-secondmost_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-secondmost-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> It is the official language of the Indian states of <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tripura" title="Tripura">Tripura</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Barak_Valley" title="Barak Valley">Barak Valley</a> of <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> \nBengali is a second official language of the <a href="/wiki/States_and_union_territories_of_India" title="States and union territories of India">Indian state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jharkhand" title="Jharkhand">Jharkhand</a> since September 2011. It is also a recognized secondary language in the City of <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a> in Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Department of Bengali in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Karachi" title="University of Karachi">University of Karachi</a> also offers regular programs of studies at the Bachelors and at the Masters levels for Bengali Literature.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The national anthems of both <a href="/wiki/Amar_Sonar_Bangla" class="mw-redirect" title="Amar Sonar Bangla">Bangladesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jana_Gana_Mana" title="Jana Gana Mana">India</a> were written in Bengali by the Bengali Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Rabindranath Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, elected representatives in both Bangladesh and West Bengal called for Bengali language to be made an <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations" title="Official languages of the United Nations">official language of the United Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bengali_dialects" title="Bengali dialects">Bengali dialects</a></div>\n<p>Regional variation in spoken Bengali constitutes a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>. Linguist <a href="/wiki/Suniti_Kumar_Chattopadhyay" class="mw-redirect" title="Suniti Kumar Chattopadhyay">Suniti Kumar Chattopadhyay</a> grouped these dialects into four large clusters\xe2\x80\x94 <a href="/wiki/Rarhi_dialect" title="Rarhi dialect">Rarh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangali_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Bangali dialect">Banga</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kamarupi_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kamarupi languages (page does not exist)">Kamarupa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Varendri_dialect" title="Varendri dialect">Varendra</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-huq_sarkar_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huq_sarkar-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> but many alternative grouping schemes have also been proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-morshed_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morshed-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The south-western dialects (<a href="/wiki/Rarhi_dialect" title="Rarhi dialect">Rarhi</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nadia_district" title="Nadia district">Nadia</a> dialect) form the basis of modern standard colloquial Bengali. In the dialects prevalent in much of eastern and south-eastern Bangladesh (<a href="/wiki/Barisal_Division" title="Barisal Division">Barisal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_Division" title="Chittagong Division">Chittagong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhaka_Division" title="Dhaka Division">Dhaka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sylhet_Division" title="Sylhet Division">Sylhet Divisions</a> of Bangladesh), many of the stops and <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">affricates</a> heard in <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a> are pronounced as <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">fricatives</a>. Western <a href="/wiki/Alveolo-palatal_consonant" title="Alveolo-palatal consonant">alveolo-palatal affricates</a> <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x9a</span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolo-palatal affricate">t\xc9\x95\xc9\x94</a>]</span>, <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x9b</span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[<a href="/wiki/Aspirated_consonant" title="Aspirated consonant">t\xc9\x95\xca\xb0\xc9\x94</a>]</span>, <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x9c</span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiced alveolo-palatal affricate">d\xca\x91\xc9\x94</a>]</span> correspond to eastern <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x9a</span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ts\xc9\x94]</span>, <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x9b</span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ts\xca\xb0\xc9\x94~s\xc9\x94]</span>, <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x9c</span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[dz\xc9\x94~z\xc9\x94]</span>. The influence of <a href="/wiki/Tibeto-Burman_languages" title="Tibeto-Burman languages">Tibeto-Burman languages</a> on the phonology of Eastern Bengali is seen through the lack of nasalized vowels and an alveolar articulation of what are categorised as the "cerebral" consonants (as opposed to the postalveolar articulation of <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>). Some variants of Bengali, particularly <a href="/wiki/Chittagonian_language" title="Chittagonian language">Chittagonian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chakma_language" title="Chakma language">Chakma</a>, have contrastive <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a>; differences in the pitch of the speaker\'s voice can distinguish words. <a href="/wiki/Rangpuri_language" title="Rangpuri language">Rangpuri</a>, Kharia Thar and Mal Paharia are closely related to Western Bengali dialects, but are typically classified as separate languages. Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Hajong_language" title="Hajong language">Hajong</a> is considered a separate language, although it shares similarities to Northern Bengali dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-hajong_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hajong-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the standardization of Bengali in the 19th century and early 20th century, the cultural center of Bengal was in the city of <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a>, founded by the British. What is accepted as the standard form today in both West Bengal and Bangladesh is based on the <a href="/wiki/Rarhi_dialect" title="Rarhi dialect">West-Central dialect</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nadia_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Nadia District">Nadia District</a>, located next to the border of Bangladesh.<sup id="cite_ref-daniul_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daniul-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> There are cases where speakers of Standard Bengali in <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a> will use a different word from a speaker of Standard Bengali in Bangladesh, even though both words are of native Bengali descent. For example, the word salt is <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\xa8\xe0\xa7\x81\xe0\xa6\xa8</span> <i>nun</i> in the west which corresponds to <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\xb2\xe0\xa6\xac\xe0\xa6\xa3</span> <i>l\xc3\xb4b\xc3\xb4n</i> in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-Banglabetelco_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banglabetelco-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spoken_and_literary_varieties">Spoken and literary varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Spoken and literary varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Bengali exhibits <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a>, though some scholars have proposed triglossia or even n-glossia or <a href="/wiki/Heteroglossia" title="Heteroglossia">heteroglossia</a> between the written and spoken forms of the language.<sup id="cite_ref-cornell_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornell-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Two styles of writing have emerged, involving somewhat different vocabularies and syntax:<sup id="cite_ref-daniul_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daniul-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-daniul1_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daniul1-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li><i><a href="/wiki/Shadhu-bhasha" title="Shadhu-bhasha">Shadhu-bhasha</a></i> (<span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\xb8\xe0\xa6\xbe\xe0\xa6\xa7\xe0\xa7\x81\xe0\xa6\xad\xe0\xa6\xbe\xe0\xa6\xb7\xe0\xa6\xbe</span> "upright language") was the written language, with longer verb inflections and more of a <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>-derived <i><a href="/wiki/Tatsama" title="Tatsama">Tatsama</a></i> vocabulary. Songs such as India\'s national anthem <i><a href="/wiki/Jana_Gana_Mana" title="Jana Gana Mana">Jana Gana Mana</a></i> (by <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Rabindranath Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a>) were composed in Shadhubhasha. However, use of Shadhubhasha in modern writing is uncommon, restricted to some official signs and documents in Bangladesh as well as for achieving particular literary effects.</li>\n<li><i>Cholito-bhasha</i> (<span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x9a\xe0\xa6\xb2\xe0\xa6\xbf\xe0\xa6\xa4\xe0\xa6\xad\xe0\xa6\xbe\xe0\xa6\xb7\xe0\xa6\xbe</span> "running language"), known by linguists as Standard Colloquial Bengali, is a written Bengali style exhibiting a preponderance of colloquial idiom and shortened verb forms, and is the standard for written Bengali now. This form came into vogue towards the turn of the 19th century, promoted by the writings of <a href="/wiki/Peary_Chand_Mitra" title="Peary Chand Mitra">Peary Chand Mitra</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Alaler_Gharer_Dulal" title="Alaler Gharer Dulal">Alaler Gharer Dulal</a></i>, 1857),<sup id="cite_ref-alal_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alal-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pramatha_Chaudhuri" title="Pramatha Chaudhuri">Pramatha Chaudhuri</a> (<i>Sabujpatra</i>, 1914) and in the later writings of <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Rabindranath Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a>. It is modeled on the dialect spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Shantipur" title="Shantipur">Shantipur</a> region in <a href="/wiki/Nadia_district" title="Nadia district">Nadia district</a>, West Bengal. This form of Bengali is often referred to as the "Nadia standard", "Nadia dialect", "Southwestern/West-Central dialect" or "Shantipuri Bangla".<sup id="cite_ref-morshed_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morshed-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p>Linguist <a href="/wiki/Prabhat_Ranjan_Sarkar" title="Prabhat Ranjan Sarkar">Prabhat Ranjan Sarkar</a>, categorizes the language as:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Madhya R\xc4\x81dhi dialect</li>\n<li>Kanthi (Contai) dialect</li>\n<li>Kolkata dialect</li>\n<li>Shantipuri (Nadia) dialect</li>\n<li>Maldahiya (Jangipuri) dialect</li>\n<li>Barendri dialect</li>\n<li>Rangpuriya dialect</li>\n<li>Sylheti dialect</li>\n<li>Dhakaiya (Bikrampuri) dialect</li>\n<li>Jessor/ Jessoriya dialect</li>\n<li>Barisal (Chandradwip) dialect</li>\n<li>Chattal (Chittagong) dialect</li></ul>\n<p><br /> \nWhile most writing is in Standard Colloquial Bengali (SCB), spoken dialects exhibit a greater variety. People in southeastern West Bengal, including Kolkata, speak in SCB. Other dialects, with minor variations from Standard Colloquial, are used in other parts of West Bengal and western Bangladesh, such as the <a href="/wiki/Midnapore" title="Midnapore">Midnapore</a> dialect, characterised by some unique words and constructions. However, a majority in Bangladesh speak in dialects notably different from SCB. Some dialects, particularly those of the <a href="/wiki/Chittagong" title="Chittagong">Chittagong</a> region, bear only a superficial resemblance to SCB.<sup id="cite_ref-ray89_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ray89-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The dialect in the Chittagong region is least widely understood by the general body of Bengalis.<sup id="cite_ref-ray89_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ray89-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The majority of Bengalis are able to communicate in more than one <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">variety</a>\xe2\x80\x94often, speakers are fluent in <i>Cholitobhasha</i> (SCB) and one or more regional dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-SKumar_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SKumar-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even in SCB, the vocabulary may differ according to the speaker\'s religion: Hindus are more likely to use <a href="/wiki/Tatsama" title="Tatsama">words derived from Sanskrit</a> and of Austroasiatic <i>Deshi</i> origin whereas Muslims are more likely to use words of Persian and Arabic origin respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-ray80_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ray80-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> For example:<sup id="cite_ref-Banglabetelco_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banglabetelco-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Predominantly Hindu usage</th>\n<th>Predominantly Muslim <i>bati</i>\n</td>\n<td>light<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bengali_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bengali_phonology" title="Bengali phonology">Bengali phonology</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemic</a> inventory of standard Bengali consists of 29 consonants and 7 vowels, as well as 7 <a href="/wiki/Nasalization" title="Nasalization">nasalized vowels</a>. The inventory is set out below in the <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">International Phonetic Alphabet</a> (upper grapheme in each box) and romanization (lower grapheme).\n</p>\n<table class="IPA">\xc9\xbd~\xc9\xbd\xca\xb1</span></td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Bengali is known for its wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Diphthongs" class="mw-redirect" title="Diphthongs">diphthongs</a>, combinations of <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a> occurring within the same <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Two of these, /<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">oi\xcc\xaf</span>/ and /<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ou\xcc\xaf</span>/, are the only ones with representation in script, as <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x90</span> and <span lang="bn" title="Bengali language text">\xe0\xa6\x94</span> respectively. /<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">e\xcc\xaf i\xcc\xaf o\xcc\xaf u\xcc\xaf</span>/ may all form the glide part of a diphthong. The total number of diphthongs is not established, with bounds at 17 and 31. An incomplete chart is given by Sarkar (1985) of the following:<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th></th>\n<th><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">e\xcc\xaf</span></th>\n<th><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">i\xcc\xaf</span></th>\n<th><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">o\xcc\xaf</span></th>\n<th><span
